Absolutely ‚Äî here are the **learning tiers** we‚Äôve been using for this course, from least to most advanced, with what each tier means in practice.

***

# Learning Tiers (HS ‚Üí PhD)

## **Tier 1 ‚Äî High School (HS) prerequisites**

**Goal:** Build the *intuition tools* needed for amplitudes and interference.  
**Core competencies:**

*   Complex numbers as ‚Äú2D arrows‚Äù: $$a+ib$$, magnitude $$|z|$$, phase $$\arg z$$
*   **Magnitude squared** as probability: $$P \propto |A|^2$$
*   **Vector addition analogy**: adding complex amplitudes before squaring ‚Üí interference
*   Basic probability: events, normalization (‚Äútotal probability = 1‚Äù)

**You should be able to:**

*   Compute $$|a+ib|^2$$
*   Understand why $$|A_1+A_2|^2\neq |A_1|^2+|A_2|^2$$ in general

***

## **Tier 2 ‚Äî Beginning Undergraduate (BegUG)**

**Goal:** Become fluent in the *working language* of bras, kets, bases, and probabilities.  
**Core competencies:**

*   Normalization: $$\langle \psi|\psi\rangle=1$$
*   Orthonormal bases: $$\langle m|n\rangle=\delta_{mn}$$
*   Components as inner products:
    $$
    c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle
    $$
*   Probabilities:
    $$
    P(n)=|c_n|^2
    $$
*   ‚ÄúInsert identity‚Äù in discrete form:
    $$
    \sum_n |n\rangle\langle n|=\mathbb I
    $$

**You should be able to:**

*   Expand a state in a basis and compute probabilities
*   Translate between $$|\psi\rangle$$ and its coordinate column vector in a chosen basis

***

## **Tier 3 ‚Äî Advanced Undergraduate (AdvUG)**

**Goal:** Understand why the formalism is *internally consistent* and geometric.  
**Core competencies:**

*   **Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz inequality**:
    $$
    |\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|^2\le \langle\phi|\phi\rangle\langle\psi|\psi\rangle
    $$
*   **Triangle inequality** for norms:
    $$
    \|\phi+\psi\|\le \|\phi\|+\|\psi\|
    $$
*   Metric/geometric viewpoint:
    *   overlaps behave like cosines of angles (up to phase)
    *   distance notions emerge from $$\|\phi-\psi\|$$ (with caveats about phase)

**You should be able to:**

*   Prove basic inequalities in Hilbert space
*   Explain why overlaps and probabilities are bounded by 1 for normalized states
*   See superposition as linear structure, not ‚Äúmystery‚Äù

***

## **Tier 4 ‚Äî Master‚Äôs (MSc)**

**Goal:** Shift from ‚Äúvectors as states‚Äù to ‚Äúrays as states‚Äù and connect to symmetry theory.  
**Core competencies:**

*   **Rays / projective Hilbert space** viewpoint:
    *   physical states are equivalence classes $$ |\psi\rangle\sim e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$$
    *   mathematically, pure states are points in **projective space** $$\mathbb P(\mathcal H)$$
*   **Wigner theorem preview**:
    *   transformations preserving transition probabilities
        $$|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|^2$$
        are implemented by **unitary or antiunitary** operators

**You should be able to:**

*   Explain why global phase is unphysical and why rays are the correct objects
*   State what Wigner‚Äôs theorem says and why it matters for symmetries

***

## **Tier 5 ‚Äî PhD**

**Goal:** Make all foundational statements **mathematically precise** and know where physics shorthand hides subtleties.  
**Core competencies:**

*   Precise definition of **Hilbert space** (inner product + completeness)
*   **Separability** (existence of a countable orthonormal basis / countable dense subset) and why it‚Äôs assumed in most QM
*   Rays as **1D subspaces** (not just ‚Äúvectors up to phase‚Äù)
*   Awareness of operator subtleties:
    *   Hermitian vs self-adjoint (domains matter)
    *   generalized eigenvectors and distributional states (preview for rigged Hilbert spaces)

**You should be able to:**

*   Distinguish informal bra‚Äìket manipulations from rigorous operator statements
*   Discuss why separability matters for spectral decompositions and expansions
*   Frame state space as a projective object and symmetry as structure on that space

***

# One-line summary of the ladder

*   **HS:** amplitudes are complex arrows ‚Üí probabilities are squared lengths
*   **BegUG:** bases/components ‚Üí compute probabilities with $$\langle n|\psi\rangle$$
*   **AdvUG:** inequalities ‚Üí geometry and consistency of the formalism
*   **MSc:** rays/projective space ‚Üí symmetries (Wigner theorem)
*   **PhD:** rigorous Hilbert space + separability + domain subtleties

***

If you want, I can also provide a **tier-to-tier mapping** (what exact skills unlock the next tier) and a **diagnostic quiz** to place a student into the right tier quickly.

Yes ‚Äî and it‚Äôs a *really* good idea. What you‚Äôre asking for is essentially an **assessment artifact taxonomy + spec** that we can reuse for **every lecture** and for **each learning tier (HS ‚Üí PhD)**, while still supporting:

*   a **simple track** (skills + fluency),
*   a **complex track** (proof + synthesis),
*   and a **research track** (reading + proposing).

Below is a **clean, reusable definition** of the artifact set, with **templates**, **rubrics**, **recommended counts**, and **how they scale by tier**. You can drop this into a course admin pack as the ‚ÄúAssessment Policy & Templates‚Äù document.

# Assessment Artifact Set (Reusable Across Lectures)

## 0) Design Goals (why this set works)

Each lecture should assess **four distinct capabilities**:

1.  **Conceptual understanding** (can you explain it?)
2.  **Procedural fluency** (can you compute it?)
3.  **Structural mastery** (can you prove/derive it?)
4.  **Transfer & creation** (can you apply it to a new context / ask new questions?)

Your six artifacts map cleanly onto these:

| Capability                   | Artifact(s)                                    |
| ---------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------- |
| Conceptual understanding     | Concept questions                              |
| Procedural fluency           | Simple problem set                             |
| Structural mastery           | Complex problem set                            |
| Transfer (engineering)       | Simple project                                 |
| Transfer (synthesis)         | Complex project                                |
| Creation & frontier thinking | Well-defined research Q; Open-ended research Q |

***

# 1) Artifact: Concept Questions (CQ)

## Purpose

Fast checks that a student has the **right mental model**, not just algebra.

## Deliverable format

*   6‚Äì10 short questions (mix of multiple-choice, true/false + justify, and 2‚Äì3 sentence prompts)
*   Designed for **10‚Äì15 minutes** in class or as pre-lecture warmup.

## Recommended counts by tier

*   **HS:** 6 (mostly qualitative)
*   **BegUG:** 8 (qualitative + very short computations)
*   **AdvUG:** 10 (include ‚Äúexplain why‚Äù)
*   **MSc/PhD:** 10 (include ‚Äústate assumptions‚Äù + ‚Äúwhat fails if‚Ä¶‚Äù)

## Example CQ prompts (bra‚Äìket Lecture 1 style)

*   Why is $$|\psi\rangle$$ not ‚Äúa column vector‚Äù until a basis is chosen?
*   What is physically meaningful: $$\langle a|\psi\rangle$$ or $$|\langle a|\psi\rangle|^2$$?
*   Why does a global phase not change probabilities?
*   In what sense is ‚Äúsuperposition‚Äù basis-dependent?

## Rubric (quick)

*   **2 points** each: correct + explanation consistent
*   Total: **12‚Äì20 points**

***

# 2) Artifact: Simple Problem Set (SPS)

## Purpose

Build **fluency**: compute probabilities, normalize states, insert identity, basic basis changes.

## Deliverable format

*   8‚Äì12 problems
*   Mostly 1‚Äì5 steps each
*   Minimal proofs; focus on correct use of notation and technique.

## Suggested structure

*   **Part A: mechanics** (normalization, components, overlaps)
*   **Part B: interpretation** (what does the computed number mean?)
*   **Part C: translation** (ket ‚Üî coordinates ‚Üî wavefunction preview)

## Tier scaling

*   **HS:** numeric complex arithmetic + simple amplitude-to-probability
*   **BegUG:** normalization + components $$c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$$ + probabilities
*   **AdvUG:** include ‚Äúshow that‚Äù using completeness, but not full proofs
*   **MSc/PhD:** fewer but deeper; emphasize representation-independence checks

## Rubric

*   Correct result (**60%**)
*   Correct method and notation (**30%**)
*   Interpretation sentence (**10%**)

***

# 3) Artifact: Complex Problem Set (CPS)

## Purpose

Assess **structural mastery**: proofs, derivations, multi-step synthesis, ‚Äúwhy the formalism works.‚Äù

## Deliverable format

*   4‚Äì6 problems
*   Multi-part (a)(b)(c)
*   Often proof-based or derivation-based
*   Designed for **3‚Äì6 hours** total work.

## Canonical CPS themes in early lectures

*   Prove Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz and/or triangle inequality
*   Prove phase invariance and ray equivalence
*   Show unitaries preserve probabilities and inner products
*   Derive completeness consequences (Parseval identity)

## Tier scaling

*   **AdvUG:** full proofs with guidance allowed
*   **MSc:** proofs + conceptual interpretation + links to projective space
*   **PhD:** proofs + additional conditions (‚Äúboundedness‚Äù, ‚Äúdomains‚Äù, ‚Äúseparability‚Äù)

## Rubric

*   Correct statement of assumptions (**15%**)
*   Logical proof structure (**45%**)
*   Correct algebra/steps (**25%**)
*   Insight / interpretation (**15%**)

***

# 4) Artifact: Simple Project (SPJ)

## Purpose

Hands-on transfer: ‚ÄúI can build something that uses the formalism.‚Äù

## Scope

*   2‚Äì6 hours
*   Structured, well-scaffolded
*   Works for mixed cohorts

## Deliverable format

*   short report (1‚Äì3 pages) **and** small artifact (code / worksheet / visualization)

## Example SPJ template (your ‚Äúbasis-expansion calculator‚Äù)

**Project S: Basis Expansion Calculator**

*   Input: coefficients $$c$$ in basis $$\{|n\rangle\}$$, unitary matrix $$U$$
*   Output: new coefficients $$c' = Uc$$
*   Checks:
    *   normalization preserved
    *   probabilities sum to 1
    *   global phase invariance
*   Deliverables:
    *   code + README
    *   one worked example
    *   short explanation of what changed and what didn‚Äôt

## Rubric

*   Correctness (**40%**)
*   Verification tests included (**25%**)
*   Clarity of explanation (**25%**)
*   Reproducibility (instructions) (**10%**)

***

# 5) Artifact: Complex Project (CPJ)

## Purpose

Synthesis and conceptual transfer: ‚ÄúI can compare frameworks and defend choices.‚Äù

## Scope

*   8‚Äì15 hours
*   Open-ended but guided
*   Should produce something like a mini-essay or mini-tool with reflection.

## Example CPJ template (your ‚Äúcompare representations‚Äù)

**Project C: Compare physical meaning across representations**

*   Choose a state and represent it:
    1.  abstract ket $$|\psi\rangle$$ (representation-free)
    2.  coordinates in basis A: $$c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$$
    3.  coordinates in basis B (unitary-related)
    4.  preview: wavefunction $$\psi(x)=\langle x|\psi\rangle$$ (conceptual only early on)
*   Analyze:
    *   What is invariant (norm, transition probabilities)?
    *   What changes (coordinates, basis-dependent ‚Äúsuperposition‚Äù)?
*   Deliverables:
    *   5‚Äì8 page report
    *   appendix with computations
    *   optional code for basis transforms / plots

## Rubric

*   Technical correctness (**30%**)
*   Conceptual synthesis (**35%**)
*   Depth & structure (**25%**)
*   Originality/insight (**10%**)

***

# 6) Artifact: Well-Defined Research Question (WRQ)

## Purpose

Train ‚Äúresearch-like thinking‚Äù while still being **bounded**: clear scope, clear output.

## Scope

*   5‚Äì10 hours
*   Must be answerable with literature + calculation + interpretation.

## Deliverable format

*   2‚Äì6 page memo with:
    *   question statement
    *   method
    *   evidence
    *   conclusion
    *   5‚Äì10 references

## Examples (early bra‚Äìket)

*   ‚ÄúHow did Born‚Äôs probabilistic interpretation change the meaning of the wavefunction between 1926‚Äì1927?‚Äù
*   ‚ÄúWhat exactly does ‚Äòstate is a ray‚Äô buy us operationally? Provide examples where vectors fail but rays succeed.‚Äù

## Rubric

*   Clear scope and method (**25%**)
*   Evidence quality (**25%**)
*   Correct technical content (**25%**)
*   Quality of argument (**25%**)

***

# 7) Artifact: Open-Ended Research Question (ORQ)

## Purpose

Expose the frontier: no single correct answer; evaluates **quality of reasoning**, not correctness.

## Scope

*   10‚Äì20 hours (or ongoing)
*   Can include proposing a new definition, comparing frameworks, or designing a thought experiment.

## Deliverable format

*   4‚Äì10 page proposal-style note:
    *   motivation
    *   what‚Äôs known (brief)
    *   what you propose
    *   how you‚Äôd test/argue it
    *   risks and limitations

## Examples (early bra‚Äìket)

*   ‚ÄúWhat minimal operational axioms force complex Hilbert space rather than real?‚Äù
*   ‚ÄúCan you formulate measurement and state update without projective collapse but still reproduce observed statistics?‚Äù

## Rubric

*   Clarity + framing (**25%**)
*   Depth of engagement (**25%**)
*   Novelty and plausibility (**25%**)
*   Awareness of limitations (**25%**)

***

# 8) Packaging standard (your multi-page exercise requirement)

For any **problem** (in SPS or CPS), the official exercise packet structure is:

1.  **Problem Statement** (no hints)
2.  **Suggestions / Hints** (scaffolded)
3.  **Advanced Suggestions** (structural hints, alternative approaches)  
    4+) **Detailed Solution** (full derivation + commentary + common pitfalls)

### Implementation note (for later repo automation)

Each exercise can be stored as:

*   `ex##_problem.tex`
*   `ex##_hints.tex`
*   `ex##_adv_hints.tex`
*   `ex##_solution.tex`
    and assembled into PDFs.

***

# 9) Recommended ‚Äúper lecture‚Äù minimum bundle (practical)

For each 90-minute lecture, a sustainable package is:

*   **Concept Questions:** 8
*   **Simple Problem Set:** 10 problems
*   **Complex Problem Set:** 4 problems
*   **Projects:** 1 simple + 1 complex
*   **Research prompts:** 1 well-defined + 1 open-ended

This yields:

*   enough material for mixed cohorts
*   manageable grading load
*   consistent cadence

***

# 10) Tier mapping: what changes *without* changing the artifacts

Same artifact types at every tier; what changes is:

*   **language** (qualitative ‚Üí formal)
*   **required rigor** (compute ‚Üí prove ‚Üí state assumptions)
*   **scope** (toy model ‚Üí general theorem ‚Üí domain subtleties)

Example: ‚Äúphase invariance‚Äù

*   HS: ‚Äúwhy doesn‚Äôt the arrow‚Äôs overall rotation matter for length?‚Äù
*   BegUG: compute $$|e^{i\theta}c_n|^2$$
*   AdvUG: prove for arbitrary basis
*   MSc: interpret as ray in projective space
*   PhD: formalize rays as 1D subspaces and identify topology/structure

# Lecture 1 ‚Äî States, Preparations, and Superposition (Sakurai ‚Üî Dirac)

## 90-minute pacing

*   **0‚Äì15:** Motivation; representations vs abstract states
*   **15‚Äì45:** Kets as vectors, rays, normalization, global phase
*   **45‚Äì70:** Bras as duals (informal here), amplitudes
*   **70‚Äì90:** First translations: ket ‚Üî column vector ‚Üî wavefunction idea

## Core outcomes

Students can:

1.  Explain state as a **ray**: $$|\psi\rangle \sim e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$$
2.  Interpret **amplitudes** and compute **probabilities**
3.  Use the discrete-basis dictionary: $$c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$$

***

# A) Reference anchors + historical anchors + expansion hooks

## Reference anchors (course anchors)

*   **Dirac:** *The Principles of Quantum Mechanics* ‚Äî formal postulates, transformation theory, bra‚Äìket ethos
*   **Sakurai & Napolitano:** *Modern Quantum Mechanics* ‚Äî measurement-driven motivation, amplitudes and interpretation

## Historical anchors (for lecture narrative and reading prompts)

*   **Born (1926)** ‚Äî probability interpretation (amplitude ‚Üí probability)
*   **Dirac (1927)** ‚Äî early ‚Äúprobability amplitude / transformation‚Äù formalism
*   **Dirac (1930)** ‚Äî bra‚Äìket notation systematized in book form
*   *(Optional later)* **von Neumann (1932)** ‚Äî Hilbert space foundations, spectral theory

## Expansion hooks (to connect early to Sakurai)

Use the ‚Äústate preparation ‚Üî measurement outcomes‚Äù thread immediately:

*   Preparation procedure ‚Üí label it $$|\psi\rangle$$
*   Measurement outcomes ‚Üí basis kets $$|a_i\rangle$$
*   Probabilities ‚Üí $$P(a_i)=|\langle a_i|\psi\rangle|^2$$
*   Representation independence ‚Üí $$|\psi\rangle$$ is abstract; numbers depend on basis/measurement choice

***

# B) Learning tiers (Lecture 1 add-ons) ‚Äî with references + papers + assessments + projects + research prompts

Below, each tier includes: **references**, **historical reading**, **question set**, **SPS/CPS guidance**, **projects**, **research prompts**.

## Tier 1 ‚Äî HS (High School prerequisites)

### Focus

*   Complex numbers as arrows; $$|z|^2$$ as probability
*   Vector addition ‚Üí interference intuition

### References

*   Any standard complex numbers chapter (algebra/pre-calc)
*   Teacher note: ‚Äúphasor addition‚Äù / vector diagrams (amplitude arrows)

### Historical reading (HS-friendly)

*   Born 1926 (story-level summary only; don‚Äôt assign the paper)

### Concept questions (HS)

1.  Why can‚Äôt probabilities be negative?
2.  Why does squaring a length give a nonnegative number?
3.  How can adding two arrows make the result shorter?

### Problem sets (HS)

*   Simple: compute $$|a+ib|^2$$, add amplitudes then square
*   Complex: derive $$|1+e^{i\phi}|^2=2+2\cos\phi$$

### Projects

*   Simple: draw amplitude arrows for different phases and compare probabilities
*   Complex: plot $$2+2\cos\phi$$ and interpret maxima/minima as interference

### Research prompts

*   Well-defined: 2 pages explaining interference via arrow addition
*   Open-ended: propose an alternative mapping from amplitude to probability and test its properties

***

## Tier 2 ‚Äî BegUG (Beginning Undergraduate)

### Focus

*   Normalization, orthogonality, components $$c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$$
*   Probabilities $$P(n)=|c_n|^2$$

### References

*   Linear algebra: inner products + orthonormal bases (any text)
*   Sakurai: early chapter sections on measurement/amplitudes

### Historical reading

*   Born 1926 (guided excerpt summary)
*   Dirac early formalism (guided secondary summary)

### Concept questions (BegUG)

1.  Why is normalization required?
2.  What does orthogonality mean operationally?
3.  Why does ‚Äúsuperposition‚Äù depend on the measurement basis?

### Problem sets

*   Simple: normalization + component extraction + probabilities
*   Complex: insert identity, prove phase invariance

### Projects

*   Simple: compute basis coefficients and probabilities for random states
*   Complex: compare probability distributions under basis change

### Research prompts

*   Well-defined: short memo ‚Äúbasis choice is measurement choice‚Äù
*   Open-ended: ‚ÄúWhy complex rather than real amplitudes?‚Äù (operational argument)

***

## Tier 3 ‚Äî AdvUG (Advanced Undergraduate)

### Focus

*   Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz and triangle inequality
*   geometry/metric viewpoint: overlaps bounded

### References

*   Proof-based linear algebra / inner product spaces
*   QM text appendices on Hilbert spaces (often in Sakurai/Ballentine/Messiah)

### Historical reading

*   von Neumann 1932 (guided, selective)

### Concept questions (AdvUG)

1.  Why must $$|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle| \le 1$$ for normalized states?
2.  What does C‚ÄìS mean physically?
3.  How does geometry enter probability?

### Problem sets

*   Simple: practice inequality applications
*   Complex: full proofs + unitary invariances

### Projects

*   Simple: numerical verification of C‚ÄìS and triangle inequality on random complex vectors
*   Complex: ‚Äúdistance between states‚Äù minimizing over global phase

### Research prompts

*   Well-defined: proof note on C‚ÄìS + probability bounds
*   Open-ended: explore alternative geometries on pure-state space

***

## Tier 4 ‚Äî MSc (Master‚Äôs)

### Focus

*   Rays / projective Hilbert space
*   Wigner theorem preview (symmetry preserves transition probabilities)

### References

*   Sakurai: symmetries
*   Geometry of QM notes: Fubini‚ÄìStudy distance (optional)

### Historical reading

*   Wigner‚Äôs early symmetry work (guided reading prompt)

### Concept questions (MSc)

1.  Why are physical states rays, not vectors?
2.  What is preserved under symmetry: vectors or transition probabilities?
3.  Why does Wigner force unitary/antiunitary maps?

### Problem sets

*   Simple: phase-invariant quantities
*   Complex: Wigner theorem proof sketch + projective distance

### Projects

*   Simple: compute Fubini‚ÄìStudy distance numerically
*   Complex: mini-essay linking symmetries ‚Üî probability invariance ‚Üî (anti)unitaries

### Research prompts

*   Well-defined: reading report on Wigner theorem (5 pages)
*   Open-ended: ‚Äúderive Hilbert space structure from operational axioms‚Äù

***

## Tier 5 ‚Äî PhD

### Focus

*   Precise Hilbert space definition; separability; rays as 1D subspaces
*   Preview of domain subtleties (unbounded operators) for later lectures

### References

*   von Neumann (foundations)
*   Functional analysis for physicists (Reed‚ÄìSimon/Berezansky-style references later)

### Historical reading

*   Stone 1932 (unitary groups) ‚Äî later time evolution
*   von Neumann 1932

### Concept questions (PhD)

1.  Why is completeness required physically?
2.  Why do we usually assume separability in NRQM?
3.  Why does ‚ÄúHermitian‚Äù vs ‚Äúself-adjoint‚Äù matter?

### Problem sets

*   Simple: formalize rays and transition probability invariance
*   Complex: separability and countable basis; domain caveats (essay-style)

### Projects

*   Simple: ‚Äúrigor checklist for safe Dirac manipulations‚Äù
*   Complex: mini-survey: projective geometry in modern QM/QIT

### Research prompts

*   Well-defined: 10-page note on separability assumptions
*   Open-ended: reconstruction programs and minimal postulates

# C) Lecture notes (LaTeX sources)

## C1) Single-source lecture chapter with instructor toggle

Copy as: `L01_chapter.tex`

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\usepackage{amsmath,amssymb,physics,hyperref}
\usepackage{enumitem}
\usepackage{tcolorbox}
\usepackage{booktabs}

% Instructor toggle:
\newif\ifinstructor

\newtcolorbox{pacingbox}{colback=blue!3!white,colframe=blue!60!black,title=90-minute pacing}
\newtcolorbox{outcomesbox}{colback=green!3!white,colframe=green!50!black,title=Core outcomes}
\newtcolorbox{warnbox}{colback=red!2!white,colframe=red!60!black,title=Common pitfalls}
\newtcolorbox{mapbox}{colback=purple!2!white,colframe=purple!60!black,title=Track mapping (Sakurai $\leftrightarrow$ Dirac)}

\title{Lecture 1: States, Preparations, and Superposition\\
\large Sakurai (measurement) $\leftrightarrow$ Dirac (axioms)}
\author{}
\date{}

\begin{document}
\maketitle

\begin{pacingbox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=*]
  \item \textbf{0--15:} Motivations; representations vs abstract states
  \item \textbf{15--45:} Kets as vectors, rays, normalization, global phase
  \item \textbf{45--70:} Bras as duals (informal), amplitudes, Born rule
  \item \textbf{70--90:} Dictionary: ket $\leftrightarrow$ vector $\leftrightarrow$ wavefunction idea
\end{itemize}
\end{pacingbox}

\begin{outcomesbox}
By the end, you can:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=*]
  \item Explain rays: $|\psi\rangle\sim e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$.
  \item Compute probabilities from amplitudes: $P(a)=|\langle a|\psi\rangle|^2$.
  \item Use discrete basis dictionary: $c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{outcomesbox}

\section{Motivation: state vs representation}
A persistent confusion is to identify a state with one of its representations: a wavefunction, a column vector,
or a matrix. In Dirac notation, the state is the abstract ket $|\psi\rangle$. A representation is obtained only
after choosing a basis, typically associated with a measurement.

\begin{warnbox}
\textbf{Pitfall:} A ket is not a column vector until a basis is chosen. Changing basis changes the coordinates,
not the physical state.
\end{warnbox}

\section{Sakurai track: preparation, outcomes, and amplitudes}
\subsection{Preparation and measurement}
A preparation is a reproducible procedure. Measuring an observable $A$ yields outcomes $a_i$ with stable frequencies,
defining probabilities $P(a_i)$.

\subsection{Why amplitudes? Interference}
Experiments show that indistinguishable alternatives do not combine by adding probabilities. Instead,
complex \emph{amplitudes} add first, then probabilities are obtained by modulus-squared.

\subsection{Born rule (operational)}
We postulate complex numbers $\langle a_i|\psi\rangle$ such that
\begin{equation}
P(a_i)=|\langle a_i|\psi\rangle|^2.
\end{equation}
Normalization follows from total probability:
\begin{equation}
\sum_i |\langle a_i|\psi\rangle|^2 = 1.
\end{equation}

\section{Dirac track: Hilbert space, rays, and inner products}
\subsection{States are rays}
Physical states are rays: $|\psi\rangle \sim c|\psi\rangle$ for any nonzero complex $c$.
In particular, global phase is irrelevant: $|\psi\rangle\sim e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$.

\subsection{Orthonormal basis and completeness}
Associate a measurement with an orthonormal basis $\{|n\rangle\}$:
\[
\langle m|n\rangle=\delta_{mn},\qquad \sum_n |n\rangle\langle n|=\mathbb I.
\]

\subsection{Key derivation: components are inner products}
If $|\psi\rangle=\sum_n c_n|n\rangle$, then
\[
\langle m|\psi\rangle=\sum_n c_n\langle m|n\rangle = c_m,
\]
so $c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$.

\subsection{Global phase invariance}
If $|\psi'\rangle=e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$, then
\[
|\langle n|\psi'\rangle|^2 = |e^{i\theta}\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2 = |\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2.
\]

\section{Synthesis mapping}
\begin{mapbox}
\begin{tabular}{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Sakurai (operational)} & \textbf{Dirac (structural)}\\ \midrule
Preparation procedure & Ket $|\psi\rangle$ (ray in $\mathcal H$)\\
Outcome label $a_i$ & Basis ket $|a_i\rangle$\\
Amplitude & Inner product $\langle a_i|\psi\rangle$\\
Probability & $|\langle a_i|\psi\rangle|^2$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{mapbox}

\section{First translation dictionary}
Discrete basis: $|\psi\rangle \leftrightarrow (c_1,\dots,c_N)^T$ where $c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$.\\
Continuous preview: $\psi(x)=\langle x|\psi\rangle$ (Lecture 6).

\section{Artifacts (listed)}
See the associated Concept Questions, Problem Sets A/B, Projects, and Research prompts.

\ifinstructor
\appendix
\section{Instructor-only notes}
Emphasize deliberate repetition: operational meaning first, then axioms; end by mapping.
\fi

\end{document}
```

# E) Assessment artifacts (Lecture 1)

## E1) Concept questions (CQ) ‚Äî 10 items

1.  What is the difference between a **state** and a **representation** of a state?
2.  Why does interference imply that probabilities cannot be the primitive additive objects?
3.  What does $$\langle a|\psi\rangle$$ mean operationally?
4.  Why do we square the magnitude of an amplitude to get a probability?
5.  Why is $$|\psi\rangle\sim e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$$ physically?
6.  What does it mean that ‚Äúsuperposition is basis dependent‚Äù?
7.  Why must $$\sum_i P(a_i)=1$$?
8.  What does $$\sum_n|n\rangle\langle n|=\mathbb I$$ mean in words?
9.  Why does a global phase not affect any measurement probability?
10. How do you interpret $$c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$$ in a measurement context?

***

## E2) Problem sets (SPS + CPS)

We‚Äôll use:

*   **SPS:** 8 problems (computational fluency)
*   **CPS:** 4 problems (proof/synthesis)

### SPS list

S1 Normalize a ket in a 2D basis  
S2 Extract components $$c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$$  
S3 Compute probabilities from amplitudes  
S4 Show global phase invariance  
S5 Use completeness to prove Parseval identity  
S6 Two-path interference probability vs phase  
S7 Basis change in a two-level system  
S8 Translate ket ‚Üî column vector

### CPS list

C1 Prove Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz  
C2 Prove triangle inequality  
C3 Show unitary basis change preserves probabilities  
C4 Phase-invariant distance: $$\min_\theta \|\phi-e^{i\theta}\psi\|$$ (advanced UG/MSc)

***

## E3) Projects

### Simple project (SPJ) ‚Äî Basis-expansion calculator

**Deliverables:** code + README + 2 examples + verification checks  
**Spec:**

*   Input coefficients $$c$$ in basis A and unitary $$U$$ (A‚ÜíB)
*   Output $$c' = Uc$$
*   Verify:
    *   $$\sum |c|^2 = \sum |c'|^2$$
    *   probabilities sum to 1
    *   global phase invariance

### Complex project (CPJ) ‚Äî Compare physical meaning across representations

**Deliverables:** 5‚Äì8 page report + appendix computations (and optionally code)

*   Represent same state as:
    1.  abstract ket $$|\psi\rangle$$
    2.  coordinate vector in basis A
    3.  coordinate vector in basis B
    4.  wavefunction preview $$\psi(x)=\langle x|\psi\rangle$$ (conceptual)
*   Explain invariants vs representation-dependent quantities.

***

## E4) Research questions

### Well-defined (WRQ)

**Prompt:** How did Born‚Äôs probability interpretation (1926) influence the meaning of ‚Äúamplitude‚Äù by the time Dirac formalized transformation theory (1927‚Äì1930)?  
**Output:** 3‚Äì5 pages with citations and an annotated timeline.

### Open-ended (ORQ)

**Prompt:** Which minimal operational assumptions force complex Hilbert space (rather than real) in reconstruction programs?  
**Output:** proposal-style memo: assumptions, consequences, open gaps.

# G) Exercise packets (ALL Lecture 1 problems)

Each packet includes **Problem ‚Üí Suggestions ‚Üí Advanced suggestions ‚Üí Detailed solution**.

> You can paste each into separate files like `L01_S1.tex`, `L01_C1.tex`, etc.

## Packet S1 ‚Äî Normalization

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S1 Normalization ‚Äî Problem}
Let $\{|1\rangle,|2\rangle\}$ be orthonormal. Normalize $|\psi\rangle=|1\rangle+i|2\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Compute $\langle\psi|\psi\rangle$.
\item Divide by $\sqrt{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret normalization as $\sum_i P(i)=1$.
\item Verify by recomputing the norm after normalization.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
$\langle\psi|=\langle1|-i\langle2|$. Then
\[
\langle\psi|\psi\rangle
=(\langle1|-i\langle2|)(|1\rangle+i|2\rangle)
=\langle1|1\rangle+\langle2|2\rangle=2
\]
since cross terms vanish by orthogonality. Hence
\[
|\psi\rangle_{\text{norm}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\left(|1\rangle+i|2\rangle\right).
\]
Check: $\langle\psi_{\text{norm}}|\psi_{\text{norm}}\rangle=1$.
\end{document}
```

## Packet S2 ‚Äî Components from inner products

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S2 Components ‚Äî Problem}
Let $|\psi\rangle=\sum_n c_n|n\rangle$ in an orthonormal basis $\{|n\rangle\}$. Prove $c_m=\langle m|\psi\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Multiply the expansion by $\langle m|$ from the left.
\item Use $\langle m|n\rangle=\delta_{mn}$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret $\langle m|\psi\rangle$ as a coordinate (projection).
\item Compare to dot products in $\mathbb C^N$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\langle m|\psi\rangle=\left\langle m\middle|\sum_n c_n|n\rangle\right\rangle
=\sum_n c_n\langle m|n\rangle
=\sum_n c_n\delta_{mn}
=c_m.
\]
\end{document}
```

## Packet S3 ‚Äî Probabilities from amplitudes

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S3 Probabilities ‚Äî Problem}
In an orthonormal basis, let $|\psi\rangle=\frac{1}{\sqrt5}(|1\rangle+2i|2\rangle)$. Compute $P(1)$ and $P(2)$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Compute $c_1=\langle1|\psi\rangle$, $c_2=\langle2|\psi\rangle$.
\item Use $P(n)=|c_n|^2$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Check normalization: $P(1)+P(2)=1$.
\item Note phase does not affect modulus.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
$c_1=1/\sqrt5$ and $c_2=2i/\sqrt5$. Thus
\[
P(1)=|c_1|^2=\frac15,\qquad P(2)=|c_2|^2=\frac{4}{5}.
\]
\end{document}
```

## Packet S4 ‚Äî Global phase invariance

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S4 Global phase ‚Äî Problem}
Show that $P(n)=|\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2$ is invariant under $|\psi\rangle\to e^{i\theta}|\psi\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Pull out $e^{i\theta}$ using linearity in the ket.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Explain why this implies states are rays.
\item Contrast with relative phase in interference.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
|\langle n|e^{i\theta}\psi\rangle|^2
=|e^{i\theta}\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2
=|e^{i\theta}|^2|\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2
=|\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2
\]
since $|e^{i\theta}|=1$.
\end{document}
```

## Packet S5 ‚Äî Completeness (Parseval identity)

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S5 Completeness ‚Äî Problem}
Given $\sum_n |n\rangle\langle n|=\mathbb I$, prove $\langle\psi|\psi\rangle=\sum_n|\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Insert the identity between $\langle\psi|$ and $|\psi\rangle$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret as ``total probability'' in basis $\{|n\rangle\}$.
\item Recognize this as a quantum version of Parseval‚Äôs identity.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\langle\psi|\psi\rangle=\langle\psi|\mathbb I|\psi\rangle
=\left\langle\psi\middle|\sum_n|n\rangle\langle n|\middle|\psi\right\rangle
=\sum_n \langle\psi|n\rangle\langle n|\psi\rangle
=\sum_n |\langle n|\psi\rangle|^2.
\]
\end{document}
```

## Packet S6 ‚Äî Two-path interference toy model

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S6 Interference ‚Äî Problem}
Let $A(\phi)=1+e^{i\phi}$. Compute $P(\phi)=|A(\phi)|^2$ and identify maxima/minima.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use $|z|^2=zz^*$.
\item Compute $(1+e^{i\phi})(1+e^{-i\phi})$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Simplify using $e^{i\phi}+e^{-i\phi}=2\cos\phi$.
\item Interpret maxima as constructive interference, minima as destructive.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
P(\phi)=|1+e^{i\phi}|^2=(1+e^{i\phi})(1+e^{-i\phi})
=2+e^{i\phi}+e^{-i\phi}
=2+2\cos\phi.
\]
Max: $\cos\phi=1$ at $\phi=0\ (\text{mod }2\pi)$ gives $P_{\max}=4$.
Min: $\cos\phi=-1$ at $\phi=\pi\ (\text{mod }2\pi)$ gives $P_{\min}=0$.
\end{document}
```

## Packet S7 ‚Äî Basis dependence in a two-level system

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S7 Basis change ‚Äî Problem}
Let $|\psi\rangle=\alpha|0\rangle+\beta|1\rangle$ with $|\alpha|^2+|\beta|^2=1$ and
$|+\rangle=(|0\rangle+|1\rangle)/\sqrt2$, $|-\rangle=(|0\rangle-|1\rangle)/\sqrt2$.
Compute $P(+)$ and $P(-)$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Compute overlaps $\langle +|\psi\rangle$ and $\langle -|\psi\rangle$.
\item Then square magnitudes.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Note $\langle +|=(\langle0|+\langle1|)/\sqrt2$.
\item Interpret: different basis corresponds to different measurement.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\langle +|\psi\rangle=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(\langle0|+\langle1|)(\alpha|0\rangle+\beta|1\rangle)
=\frac{\alpha+\beta}{\sqrt2}.
\]
Similarly $\langle -|\psi\rangle=(\alpha-\beta)/\sqrt2$.
Thus
\[
P(+)=\frac{|\alpha+\beta|^2}{2},\qquad P(-)=\frac{|\alpha-\beta|^2}{2}.
\]
\end{document}
```

## Packet S8 ‚Äî Ket ‚Üî column vector translation

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-S8 Translation ‚Äî Problem}
In basis $\{|1\rangle,|2\rangle\}$, let $|\psi\rangle=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(|1\rangle+i|2\rangle)$.
Write the coordinate column vector and compute probabilities.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Coordinates are $c_1=\langle1|\psi\rangle$, $c_2=\langle2|\psi\rangle$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Emphasize: the column vector depends on basis choice.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
$c_1=1/\sqrt2$, $c_2=i/\sqrt2$. Column vector:
\[
\mathbf c=\begin{pmatrix}1/\sqrt2\\ i/\sqrt2\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Probabilities: $P(1)=|c_1|^2=1/2$, $P(2)=|c_2|^2=1/2$.
\end{document}
```

***

## Packet C1 ‚Äî Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-C1 Cauchy--Schwarz ‚Äî Problem}
Prove $|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|^2\le \langle\phi|\phi\rangle\langle\psi|\psi\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Consider $\|\phi-\lambda\psi\|^2\ge 0$ for all $\lambda\in\mathbb C$.
\item Expand and choose $\lambda$ to minimize.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use $\lambda=\frac{\langle\psi|\phi\rangle}{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}$ when $\psi\neq 0$.
\item Rearrange the inequality after substitution.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
Let $f(\lambda)=\|\phi-\lambda\psi\|^2=\langle\phi-\lambda\psi|\phi-\lambda\psi\rangle\ge 0$.
Expand:
\[
f(\lambda)=\langle\phi|\phi\rangle-\lambda\langle\phi|\psi\rangle-\lambda^*\langle\psi|\phi\rangle
+|\lambda|^2\langle\psi|\psi\rangle.
\]
Choose $\lambda=\frac{\langle\psi|\phi\rangle}{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}$. Then
\[
0\le f(\lambda)=\langle\phi|\phi\rangle-\frac{|\langle\psi|\phi\rangle|^2}{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}.
\]
Multiply by $\langle\psi|\psi\rangle>0$:
\[
|\langle\psi|\phi\rangle|^2\le \langle\phi|\phi\rangle\langle\psi|\psi\rangle.
\]
Since $|\langle\psi|\phi\rangle|=|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|$, we are done.
\end{document}
```

## Packet C2 ‚Äî Triangle inequality

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-C2 Triangle inequality ‚Äî Problem}
Let $\|\psi\|=\sqrt{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}$. Prove $\|\phi+\psi\|\le \|\phi\|+\|\psi\|$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Square both sides.
\item Expand $\|\phi+\psi\|^2$ and bound cross term using Cauchy--Schwarz.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use $\Re z\le |z|$.
\item Conclude by taking square roots (nonnegativity).
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\|\phi+\psi\|^2=\langle\phi+\psi|\phi+\psi\rangle
=\|\phi\|^2+\|\psi\|^2+2\Re\langle\phi|\psi\rangle.
\]
Since $\Re\langle\phi|\psi\rangle\le |\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|\le \|\phi\|\|\psi\|$ (C--S),
\[
\|\phi+\psi\|^2 \le \|\phi\|^2+\|\psi\|^2+2\|\phi\|\|\psi\|=(\|\phi\|+\|\psi\|)^2.
\]
Taking square roots yields $\|\phi+\psi\|\le \|\phi\|+\|\psi\|$.
\end{document}
```

## Packet C3 ‚Äî Unitary change-of-basis preserves probabilities

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-C3 Unitary invariance ‚Äî Problem}
Let $\mathbf c$ be coefficients of $|\psi\rangle$ in basis A. Let $\mathbf c'=U\mathbf c$ where $U^\dagger U=I$.
Show $\sum_i|c'_i|^2=\sum_i|c_i|^2$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use vector notation: $\|\mathbf c\|^2=\mathbf c^\dagger \mathbf c$.
\item Compute $\|\mathbf c'\|^2$ and insert $U^\dagger U$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret as probability conservation under basis change.
\item Connect to inner-product preservation: $\langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle=\langle\phi|\psi\rangle$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\sum_i|c'_i|^2=\|\mathbf c'\|^2=(U\mathbf c)^\dagger(U\mathbf c)=\mathbf c^\dagger U^\dagger U\mathbf c=\mathbf c^\dagger\mathbf c=\sum_i|c_i|^2.
\]
Thus normalization (and total probability) is preserved.
\end{document}
```

## Packet C4 ‚Äî Phase-invariant distance (AdvUG/MSc)

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L01-C4 Phase-invariant distance ‚Äî Problem}
Define $d(\phi,\psi)=\min_{\theta}\|\phi-e^{i\theta}\psi\|$ for normalized states.
Show $d(\phi,\psi)$ depends only on the rays and express it in terms of $|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Expand $\|\phi-e^{i\theta}\psi\|^2$.
\item Use $\Re(e^{i\theta}\langle\phi|\psi\rangle)$ and choose $\theta$ to maximize it.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Write $\langle\phi|\psi\rangle=re^{i\alpha}$ and choose $\theta=-\alpha$.
\item Conclude $d^2=2-2|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|$ for normalized states.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
Assume $\|\phi\|=\|\psi\|=1$. Then
\[
\|\phi-e^{i\theta}\psi\|^2=\langle\phi-e^{i\theta}\psi|\phi-e^{i\theta}\psi\rangle
=1+1- e^{i\theta}\langle\phi|\psi\rangle- e^{-i\theta}\langle\psi|\phi\rangle
=2-2\Re(e^{i\theta}\langle\phi|\psi\rangle).
\]
Let $\langle\phi|\psi\rangle=re^{i\alpha}$ with $r=|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|$. Then
\[
\Re(e^{i\theta}\langle\phi|\psi\rangle)=\Re(re^{i(\theta+\alpha)})=r\cos(\theta+\alpha).
\]
Maximizing occurs at $\theta=-\alpha$ giving maximum $r$. Hence
\[
d^2(\phi,\psi)=2-2r=2-2|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|.
\]
This depends only on $|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|$, which is ray-invariant.
\end{document}
```

# C) Lecture notes (Narrative + derivations)

## C1) Sakurai track ‚Äî transition amplitudes

Operationally, a transition amplitude compares two preparations:

$$
\text{prepare }|\psi\rangle \quad\text{measure outcome }|a\rangle.
$$

The complex number $$\langle a|\psi\rangle$$ encodes the probability via

$$
P(a\leftarrow\psi)=|\langle a|\psi\rangle|^2.
$$

This forces a structure:

*   linearity in the state being prepared
*   complex conjugation when reversing roles (prepare ‚Üî test)

***

## C2) Dirac track ‚Äî bras as dual vectors

### Definition (physics convention)

For every ket $$|\psi\rangle$$, there is a bra $$\langle\psi|$$ such that

$$
\langle\psi|\phi\rangle \in \mathbb C
$$

is the inner product.

### Linearity rules

*   Linear in the **ket**:

$$
\langle\phi|(a|\psi_1\rangle+b|\psi_2\rangle)
= a\langle\phi|\psi_1\rangle + b\langle\phi|\psi_2\rangle
$$

*   **Antilinear** in the bra:

$$
(a\langle\phi_1|+b\langle\phi_2|)|\psi\rangle
= a^*\langle\phi_1|\psi\rangle + b^*\langle\phi_2|\psi\rangle
$$

This is why complex conjugation appears.

***

## C3) Inner product axioms

For all $$|\phi\rangle,|\psi\rangle$$:

1.  Conjugate symmetry

$$
\langle\phi|\psi\rangle=\langle\psi|\phi\rangle^*
$$

2.  Linearity in the ket

3.  Positivity

$$
\langle\psi|\psi\rangle \ge 0,\quad =0 \iff |\psi\rangle=0
$$

Define norm:

$$
\|\psi\|=\sqrt{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}
$$

***

## C4) Geometry and overlaps

For normalized states,

$$
|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle| \le 1
$$

(guaranteed by Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz).

*   $$|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|=1$$: same ray
*   $$|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|=0$$: orthogonal (mutually exclusive outcomes)

***

# D) Concept Questions (CQ) ‚Äî 10

1.  Why is a bra not just a transpose of a ket?
2.  Why must complex conjugation appear in inner products?
3.  What is the operational meaning of $$\langle\phi|\psi\rangle$$?
4.  Why is $$\langle\psi|\psi\rangle$$ always real and nonnegative?
5.  What does orthogonality mean physically?
6.  Why is linearity chosen in the ket, not the bra?
7.  What changes if we swap bra and ket order?
8.  How does inner product encode probability?
9.  Why does $$|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|\le1$$ matter physically?
10. How does this lecture prepare for operators?

***

# K) Exercise Packets (Problem ‚Üí Hints ‚Üí Advanced ‚Üí Solution)

Below are **three representative packets**. All others follow the same format.

***

## **Packet L02‚ÄëS2 ‚Äî Normalization via inner product**

### Problem

Normalize $$|\psi\rangle = (1+i)|1\rangle + 2|2\rangle$$.

### Suggestions

*   Compute $$\langle\psi|\psi\rangle$$.
*   Divide by $$\sqrt{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}$$.

### Advanced suggestions

*   Track complex conjugation carefully.
*   Interpret normalization probabilistically.

### Detailed solution

$$
\langle\psi|\psi\rangle
=(1-i)(1+i)\langle1|1\rangle +4\langle2|2\rangle
=2+4=6.
$$

Thus

$$
|\psi\rangle_{\text{norm}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt6}\big((1+i)|1\rangle+2|2\rangle\big).
$$

***

## **Packet L02‚ÄëC2 ‚Äî Cauchy‚ÄìSchwarz**

### Problem

Prove

$$
|\langle\phi|\psi\rangle|^2 \le \langle\phi|\phi\rangle\langle\psi|\psi\rangle.
$$

### Suggestions

*   Consider $$\|\phi-\lambda\psi\|^2\ge0$$.

### Advanced suggestions

*   Choose $$\lambda=\frac{\langle\psi|\phi\rangle}{\langle\psi|\psi\rangle}$$.

### Detailed solution

*(Identical to Lecture 1, but now interpreted as a property of inner products themselves.)*

***

## **Packet L02‚ÄëS6 ‚Äî Bra‚Äìket ‚Üî matrix translation**

### Problem

Let

$$
|\psi\rangle=\begin{pmatrix}1\\ i\end{pmatrix}.
$$

Write $$\langle\psi|$$ and compute $$\langle\psi|\psi\rangle$$.

### Suggestions

*   Take conjugate transpose.

### Advanced suggestions

*   Compare with Euclidean dot product.

### Detailed solution

$$
\langle\psi|=(1,-i),\quad
\langle\psi|\psi\rangle=1+1=2.
$$

# C) Lecture notes (Narrative + derivations) ‚Äî LaTeX sources

## C1) Single-source chapter with instructor toggle

Copy as: `L03_chapter.tex`

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\usepackage{amsmath,amssymb,physics,hyperref}
\usepackage{enumitem}
\usepackage{tcolorbox}
\usepackage{booktabs}

\newif\ifinstructor

\newtcolorbox{pacingbox}{colback=blue!3!white,colframe=blue!60!black,title=90-minute pacing}
\newtcolorbox{outcomesbox}{colback=green!3!white,colframe=green!50!black,title=Core outcomes}
\newtcolorbox{warnbox}{colback=red!2!white,colframe=red!60!black,title=Common pitfalls}
\newtcolorbox{mapbox}{colback=purple!2!white,colframe=purple!60!black,title=Abstract $\leftrightarrow$ Matrix dictionary}
\newtcolorbox{sakbox}{colback=orange!3!white,colframe=orange!70!black,title=Sakurai track (measurement-driven)}
\newtcolorbox{dirbox}{colback=gray!3!white,colframe=gray!60!black,title=Dirac track (axiomatic)}

\title{Lecture 3: Operators and Observables\\
\large Abstract operators and matrix representations side-by-side}
\author{}
\date{}

\begin{document}
\maketitle

\begin{pacingbox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=*]
\item 0--15: Observables as measurement procedures; expectation values
\item 15--35: Operators acting on kets; matrix elements $\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle$
\item 35--55: Adjoint, Hermitian operators; reality of expectation values
\item 55--70: Unitary operators; symmetries vs basis changes
\item 70--90: Worked 2D examples (Pauli matrices), projectors, identity insertions
\end{itemize}
\end{pacingbox}

\begin{outcomesbox}
By the end, you can:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=*]
\item Compute matrix elements $\langle\phi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$ and translate to matrices in a basis.
\item Define $\hat A^\dagger$ and identify Hermitian ($\hat A=\hat A^\dagger$) and unitary ($\hat U^\dagger\hat U=I$).
\item Compute expectation values $\langle\hat A\rangle_\psi=\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$ and show they are real for Hermitian $\hat A$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{outcomesbox}

\section{Motivation: from measurement outcomes to operators}
\begin{sakbox}
In the lab, an \emph{observable} is a measurement procedure that outputs a real number.
If we prepare a state $|\psi\rangle$ and repeat the measurement of $A$, we obtain a distribution of outcomes.
The \emph{expectation value} is the average outcome:
\[
\langle A\rangle_\psi = \sum_i a_i\,P(a_i|\psi).
\]
We want a compact, representation-independent way to compute this.
\end{sakbox}

\section{Operators: abstract action and matrix representation}
\begin{dirbox}
An operator $\hat A$ is a linear map $\mathcal H\to\mathcal H$.
It takes a state to a new state: $|\psi\rangle \mapsto \hat A|\psi\rangle$.
\end{dirbox}

\subsection{Matrix elements and the bridge to matrices}
Choose an orthonormal basis $\{|n\rangle\}$.
Define the \emph{matrix elements} of $\hat A$ by
\[
A_{mn}=\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle.
\]
Then for any $|\psi\rangle=\sum_n c_n|n\rangle$, we have
\[
\hat A|\psi\rangle=\sum_{m,n} |m\rangle\,A_{mn}\,c_n,
\]
so the coefficient vector transforms as
\[
\mathbf c' = A\,\mathbf c,
\]
where $A$ is the matrix with entries $A_{mn}$.
This is exactly matrix mechanics.

\begin{warnbox}
A matrix is a \emph{representation} of an operator in a chosen basis.
Changing basis changes the matrix, not the abstract operator.
\end{warnbox}

\subsection{Expectation values in bra--ket and in matrices}
Define
\[
\langle \hat A\rangle_\psi = \langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle.
\]
In the basis $\{|n\rangle\}$ this becomes
\[
\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle
= \sum_{m,n} c_m^* A_{mn} c_n
= \mathbf c^\dagger A \mathbf c.
\]

\section{Adjoint and Hermitian operators}
The adjoint $\hat A^\dagger$ is defined by
\[
\langle\phi|\hat A|\psi\rangle = \langle \hat A^\dagger \phi|\psi\rangle
\quad\text{for all }|\phi\rangle,|\psi\rangle.
\]
In a basis, $(A^\dagger)_{mn}=A_{nm}^*$, i.e. $A^\dagger$ is the conjugate transpose matrix.

\subsection{Hermitian (self-adjoint) and reality of measurement outcomes}
An observable corresponds to a Hermitian operator (physics shorthand) $\hat A=\hat A^\dagger$.
Then
\[
\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle \in \mathbb R.
\]
Proof (short): take complex conjugate and use Hermiticity.

\section{Unitary operators: symmetries and basis changes}
A unitary operator $\hat U$ satisfies $\hat U^\dagger\hat U=I$.
It preserves inner products:
\[
\langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle = \langle\phi|\psi\rangle,
\]
so it preserves norms and probabilities.

\subsection{Basis change vs physical transformation}
If we \emph{change basis} by a unitary $U$, the matrix of an operator transforms by similarity:
\[
A' = UAU^\dagger.
\]
If we \emph{physically transform the state}, we write
\[
|\psi'\rangle = U|\psi\rangle.
\]
Both involve $U$, but they represent different conceptual moves.

\begin{mapbox}
\begin{tabular}{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Abstract} & \textbf{Matrix in basis } $\{|n\rangle\}$\\ \midrule
$|\psi\rangle$ & column vector $\mathbf c$ with $c_n=\langle n|\psi\rangle$\\
$\hat A$ & matrix $A$ with $A_{mn}=\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle$\\
$\hat A|\psi\rangle$ & $\mathbf c' = A\mathbf c$\\
$\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$ & $\mathbf c^\dagger A \mathbf c$\\
$\hat A^\dagger$ & conjugate transpose $A^\dagger$\\
unitary $\hat U$ & $U^\dagger U=I$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{mapbox}

\section{Worked example: 2D system and Pauli matrices}
Let $\{|0\rangle,|1\rangle\}$ be a basis and consider
\[
\sigma_x=\begin{pmatrix}0&1\\1&0\end{pmatrix},\quad
\sigma_z=\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}.
\]
These are Hermitian. For $|\psi\rangle=\alpha|0\rangle+\beta|1\rangle$,
\[
\langle\sigma_z\rangle = |\alpha|^2-|\beta|^2.
\]
Projector onto $|0\rangle$ is $P_0=|0\rangle\langle0|$ with matrix $\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&0\end{pmatrix}$.

\ifinstructor
\appendix
\section{Instructor-only notes}
Emphasize the dual role of $U$: (i) change of basis $A'\!=\!UAU^\dagger$ vs (ii) active transformation $|\psi'\rangle\!=\!U|\psi\rangle$.
Connect expectation value to ensemble average and spectral decomposition preview.
\fi

\end{document}
```

```latex
\documentclass{beamer}
\usetheme{Madrid}
\usepackage{amsmath}
\title{Lecture 3: Operators and Observables (Abstract $\leftrightarrow$ Matrix)}
\begin{document}

\begin{frame}\titlepage\end{frame}

\begin{frame}{Roadmap}
\begin{itemize}
\item Observables as measurements (Sakurai)
\item Operators acting on kets (Dirac)
\item Matrices as representations: $A_{mn}=\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle$
\item Hermitian and unitary operators
\end{itemize}
\end{frame}

\begin{frame}{Dictionary}
\[
A_{mn}=\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle,\quad
\mathbf c' = A\mathbf c,\quad
\langle\hat A\rangle=\mathbf c^\dagger A\mathbf c
\]
\end{frame}

\begin{frame}{Hermitian and unitary}
\begin{itemize}
\item Hermitian: $\hat A=\hat A^\dagger \Rightarrow \langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle\in\mathbb R$
\item Unitary: $U^\dagger U=I \Rightarrow \langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle=\langle\phi|\psi\rangle$
\end{itemize}
\end{frame}

\begin{frame}{Example: Pauli matrices}
\[
\sigma_x=\begin{pmatrix}0&1\\1&0\end{pmatrix},\quad
\sigma_z=\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}
\]
\end{frame}
```

***

# E) Concept Questions (CQ) ‚Äî 10

1.  What is the difference between an observable and an operator?
2.  Why is a matrix not ‚Äúthe operator‚Äù until a basis is chosen?
3.  What does $$\langle\phi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$$ mean operationally?
4.  What is an adjoint operator and why does conjugation appear?
5.  Why must observables correspond to Hermitian (self-adjoint) operators?
6.  Why is $$\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$$ real for Hermitian $$\hat A$$?
7.  What does a unitary operator preserve?
8.  How do you distinguish a basis change from a physical symmetry?
9.  What is a projector and what does it represent physically?
10. How does this lecture prepare for commutators and uncertainty?

***

# F) **Simple Problem Set (SPS) ‚Äî 10**

**S1** Compute matrix elements from an operator expansion  
**S2** Compute operator action $$\hat A|\psi\rangle$$ and represent it as matrix multiplication  
**S3** Compute expectation value in bra‚Äìket form and matrix form  
**S4** Compute adjoint of a given matrix and verify adjoint property with inner products  
**S5** Check whether a matrix is Hermitian; compute expectation value and verify it‚Äôs real  
**S6** Check whether a matrix is unitary; verify norm preservation on a state  
**S7** Build a projector $$|a\rangle\langle a|$$ and verify idempotence  
**S8** Use identity insertion to derive $$\langle\phi|\hat A|\psi\rangle=\sum_{mn}\phi_m^*A_{mn}\psi_n$$  
**S9** Perform a basis change $$A'=UAU^\dagger$$ for a 2√ó2 example  
**S10** Pauli example: compute $$\langle\sigma_z\rangle$$ for $$|\psi\rangle=\alpha|0\rangle+\beta|1\rangle$$

***

# G) **Complex Problem Set (CPS) ‚Äî 5**

**C1** Prove: if $$\hat A=\hat A^\dagger$$, then $$\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle\in\mathbb R$$  
**C2** Prove: if $$U$$ is unitary, then $$\langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle=\langle\phi|\psi\rangle$$  
**C3** Prove: under basis change $$ |n'\rangle = \sum_m U_{mn}|m\rangle$$, operator matrix transforms as $$A'=U A U^\dagger$$  
**C4** Show: projectors satisfy $$P^2=P$$ and are Hermitian; interpret physically  
**C5 (MSc/PhD extension)** Hermitian vs self-adjoint: short structured essay with an example of symmetric ‚âÝ self-adjoint (no heavy machinery required; focus on meaning)

***

# H) Projects

## Simple Project (SPJ) ‚Äî ‚ÄúOperator/Matrices calculator‚Äù

Implement a small tool that:

*   inputs a 2√ó2 or 3√ó3 complex matrix $$A$$ and state vector $$c$$
*   outputs $$Ac$$, $$\langle A\rangle=c^\dagger A c$$
*   checks whether $$A$$ is Hermitian/unitary (numerically)
*   verifies $$\langle A\rangle$$ is (numerically) real if Hermitian

**Deliverables:** code + README + 3 test cases + short explanation.

## Complex Project (CPJ) ‚Äî ‚ÄúPassive vs active transformation lab‚Äù

Pick one nontrivial unitary $$U$$. Do two experiments:

1.  **Passive:** change basis and compute $$A'=UAU^\dagger$$ and $$c'=Uc$$ (coordinates)
2.  **Active:** physically transform the state $$|\psi'\rangle=U|\psi\rangle$$ and compute probabilities

Write a 6‚Äì10 page report clarifying:

*   what changes (coordinates/matrix entries)
*   what stays invariant (probabilities, inner products)
*   where students commonly confuse the two.

**How did Heisenberg‚Äôs matrix mechanics (1925) and Dirac‚Äôs transformation theory (1927) converge into the modern operator formalism?**  
Output: 4‚Äì6 page annotated narrative + 6 references.

## Open-ended (ORQ)

**Is the operator formalism inevitable from operational measurement principles?**  
Propose a reconstruction path: assumptions, predicted structure, what could vary.

## **S1 ‚Äî Matrix elements from bra‚Äìket**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S1 Matrix elements ‚Äî Problem}
Let $\{|0\rangle,|1\rangle\}$ be an orthonormal basis. Define
\[
\hat A = 2|0\rangle\langle 0| + (1+i)|0\rangle\langle 1| + (1-i)|1\rangle\langle 0| - |1\rangle\langle 1|.
\]
Compute the matrix $A_{mn}=\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle$ in this basis.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use $\langle m|\,|a\rangle\langle b|\,|n\rangle=\langle m|a\rangle\langle b|n\rangle$.
\item Orthonormality gives $\langle m|n\rangle=\delta_{mn}$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Recognize that $|0\rangle\langle1|$ contributes only to the $(0,1)$ entry.
\item Write the matrix by inspecting each dyad $|m\rangle\langle n|$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
Compute entries:
\[
A_{00}=\langle0|\hat A|0\rangle=2,\quad
A_{01}=\langle0|\hat A|1\rangle=1+i,
\]
\[
A_{10}=\langle1|\hat A|0\rangle=1-i,\quad
A_{11}=\langle1|\hat A|1\rangle=-1.
\]
Thus
\[
A=\begin{pmatrix}2 & 1+i\\ 1-i & -1\end{pmatrix}.
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **S2 ‚Äî Operator action as matrix multiplication**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S2 Action ‚Äî Problem}
Let $A=\begin{pmatrix}2 & 1+i\\ 1-i & -1\end{pmatrix}$ and $c=\begin{pmatrix}\alpha\\ \beta\end{pmatrix}$.
Compute $c'=Ac$ and interpret $c'$ as the coefficients of $\hat A|\psi\rangle$ in the basis.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Multiply the matrix by the vector.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Connect to $\hat A|\psi\rangle=\sum_m |m\rangle \sum_n A_{mn}c_n$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
c'=\begin{pmatrix}2 & 1+i\\ 1-i & -1\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}\alpha\\ \beta\end{pmatrix}
=\begin{pmatrix}2\alpha+(1+i)\beta\\ (1-i)\alpha-\beta\end{pmatrix}.
\]
These are the coefficients of $\hat A|\psi\rangle$ in the chosen basis.
\end{document}
```

***

## **S3 ‚Äî Expectation value (bra‚Äìket ‚Üî matrix)**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S3 Expectation value ‚Äî Problem}
Let $A=\begin{pmatrix}2 & 1+i\\ 1-i & -1\end{pmatrix}$ and normalized $c=\begin{pmatrix}\alpha\\ \beta\end{pmatrix}$.
Compute $\langle A\rangle=c^\dagger A c$ explicitly.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item First compute $Ac$.
\item Then compute $c^\dagger(Ac)$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Write $c^\dagger=(\alpha^*,\beta^*)$.
\item Keep track of complex conjugates.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
Ac=\begin{pmatrix}2\alpha+(1+i)\beta\\ (1-i)\alpha-\beta\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
\langle A\rangle = (\alpha^*,\beta^*)\begin{pmatrix}2\alpha+(1+i)\beta\\ (1-i)\alpha-\beta\end{pmatrix}
=2|\alpha|^2-\;|\beta|^2 + \alpha^*(1+i)\beta + \beta^*(1-i)\alpha.
\]
Note the last two terms are complex conjugates, so their sum is $2\Re\!\big((1+i)\alpha^*\beta\big)$:
\[
\boxed{\langle A\rangle = 2|\alpha|^2-|\beta|^2 + 2\Re\!\big((1+i)\alpha^*\beta\big).}
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **S4 ‚Äî Adjoint and adjoint property**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S4 Adjoint ‚Äî Problem}
Let $A=\begin{pmatrix}0 & 2+i\\ 1 & 3i\end{pmatrix}$.
(i) Compute $A^\dagger$. (ii) Verify for vectors $x,y\in\mathbb C^2$ that $x^\dagger(Ay)=(A^\dagger x)^\dagger y$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item $A^\dagger$ is conjugate transpose.
\item Use associativity of matrix multiplication.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Write $x^\dagger(Ay)=(x^\dagger A)y$ and compare with $(A^\dagger x)^\dagger y$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
(i) Transpose and conjugate:
\[
A^\dagger=\begin{pmatrix}0^* & 1^*\\ (2+i)^* & (3i)^*\end{pmatrix}
=\begin{pmatrix}0 & 1\\ 2-i & -3i\end{pmatrix}.
\]
(ii) Using matrix identities, $(A^\dagger x)^\dagger = x^\dagger A$ (since $(A^\dagger x)^\dagger=x^\dagger A$).
Thus
\[
(A^\dagger x)^\dagger y = (x^\dagger A)y = x^\dagger(Ay),
\]
which verifies the adjoint property.
\end{document}
```

***

## **S5 ‚Äî Hermitian check + reality of expectation**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S5 Hermitian ‚Äî Problem}
Let $A=\begin{pmatrix}2 & 1+i\\ 1-i & -1\end{pmatrix}$.
(i) Show $A$ is Hermitian. (ii) Using $c=(\alpha,\beta)^T$, show $\langle A\rangle=c^\dagger A c$ is real.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Compute $A^\dagger$ and compare to $A$.
\item Use the explicit form from S3.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Note that $c^\dagger A c$ equals its own complex conjugate if $A=A^\dagger$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
(i) $A^\dagger=\begin{pmatrix}2 & (1-i)^*\\ (1+i)^* & -1\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}2 & 1+i\\ 1-i & -1\end{pmatrix}=A$.
(ii) From S3:
\[
\langle A\rangle = 2|\alpha|^2-|\beta|^2 + \alpha^*(1+i)\beta + \beta^*(1-i)\alpha.
\]
The last two terms are complex conjugates, hence their sum is $2\Re((1+i)\alpha^*\beta)$.
So $\langle A\rangle\in\mathbb R$.
\end{document}
```

***

## **S6 ‚Äî Unitary check + norm preservation**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S6 Unitary ‚Äî Problem}
Let $U=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\begin{pmatrix}1&1\\1&-1\end{pmatrix}$.
(i) Show $U^\dagger U=I$. (ii) Show $\|Uc\|=\|c\|$ for any $c\in\mathbb C^2$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Compute $U^\dagger$ and multiply.
\item Use $\|c\|^2=c^\dagger c$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item For (ii): $(Uc)^\dagger(Uc)=c^\dagger(U^\dagger U)c$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
(i) $U$ is real symmetric, so $U^\dagger=U^T=U$. Then
\[
U^\dagger U = \frac{1}{2}\begin{pmatrix}1&1\\1&-1\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}1&1\\1&-1\end{pmatrix}
=\frac{1}{2}\begin{pmatrix}2&0\\0&2\end{pmatrix}=I.
\]
(ii)
\[
\|Uc\|^2=(Uc)^\dagger(Uc)=c^\dagger(U^\dagger U)c=c^\dagger c=\|c\|^2.
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **S7 ‚Äî Projector construction + idempotence**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S7 Projector ‚Äî Problem}
Let $|a\rangle$ be normalized. Define $P=|a\rangle\langle a|$.
Show (i) $P^2=P$, (ii) $P^\dagger=P$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Multiply $P$ by itself and use $\langle a|a\rangle=1$.
\item Use $(|a\rangle\langle a|)^\dagger=|a\rangle\langle a|$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret $P$ as ‚Äúfilter that keeps the component along $|a\rangle$‚Äù.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
(i)
\[
P^2=(|a\rangle\langle a|)(|a\rangle\langle a|)=|a\rangle(\langle a|a\rangle)\langle a|=|a\rangle\langle a|=P.
\]
(ii)
\[
P^\dagger=(|a\rangle\langle a|)^\dagger=|a\rangle\langle a|=P.
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **S8 ‚Äî Identity insertion gives matrix formula**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S8 Identity insertion ‚Äî Problem}
Let $|\psi\rangle=\sum_n \psi_n|n\rangle$ and $|\phi\rangle=\sum_m \phi_m|m\rangle$.
Show
\[
\langle\phi|\hat A|\psi\rangle=\sum_{m,n}\phi_m^*\,A_{mn}\,\psi_n
\]
where $A_{mn}=\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Insert $\sum_m|m\rangle\langle m|$ and $\sum_n|n\rangle\langle n|$.
\item Use $\langle\phi|m\rangle=\phi_m^*$ and $\langle n|\psi\rangle=\psi_n$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Recognize this as the abstract derivation of $\phi^\dagger A\psi$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
Insert identities:
\[
\langle\phi|\hat A|\psi\rangle
=\sum_{m,n}\langle\phi|m\rangle\langle m|\hat A|n\rangle\langle n|\psi\rangle
=\sum_{m,n}\phi_m^*\,A_{mn}\,\psi_n.
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **S9 ‚Äî Basis change $$A'=UAU^\dagger$$ in 2√ó2**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S9 Basis change ‚Äî Problem}
Let $A=\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}$ and $U=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\begin{pmatrix}1&1\\1&-1\end{pmatrix}$.
Compute $A'=UAU^\dagger$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Note $U^\dagger=U$ here.
\item Multiply step by step: first $B=UA$, then $A'=BU$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret $A'$ as the matrix of the same abstract operator in the new basis.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
Compute:
\[
UA=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\begin{pmatrix}1&1\\1&-1\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}
=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\begin{pmatrix}1&-1\\1&1\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
A'= (UA)U = \frac{1}{2}\begin{pmatrix}1&-1\\1&1\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}1&1\\1&-1\end{pmatrix}
=\frac{1}{2}\begin{pmatrix}0&2\\2&0\end{pmatrix}
=\begin{pmatrix}0&1\\1&0\end{pmatrix}.
\]
So $A'=\sigma_x$.
\end{document}
```

***

## **S10 ‚Äî Pauli expectation**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-S10 Pauli expectation ‚Äî Problem}
Let $\sigma_z=\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}$ and $|\psi\rangle=\alpha|0\rangle+\beta|1\rangle$ with $|\alpha|^2+|\beta|^2=1$.
Compute $\langle\sigma_z\rangle=\langle\psi|\sigma_z|\psi\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use matrix form: $c=(\alpha,\beta)^T$ and compute $c^\dagger\sigma_z c$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret the result as ‚Äúbias toward outcome +1 vs ‚àí1‚Äù.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\langle\sigma_z\rangle = (\alpha^*,\beta^*)\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}\alpha\\\beta\end{pmatrix}
=(\alpha^*,\beta^*)\begin{pmatrix}\alpha\\-\beta\end{pmatrix}
=|\alpha|^2-|\beta|^2.
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **C1 ‚Äî Hermitian ‚áí real expectation (proof)**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-C1 Hermitian expectation ‚Äî Problem}
Prove that if $\hat A=\hat A^\dagger$, then for any $|\psi\rangle$, $\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle\in\mathbb R$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Take complex conjugate of $\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$.
\item Use $(\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle)^*=\langle\psi|\hat A^\dagger|\psi\rangle$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Show the quantity equals its own conjugate.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
Let $z=\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle$. Then
\[
z^* = (\langle\psi|\hat A|\psi\rangle)^* = \langle\psi|\hat A^\dagger|\psi\rangle
\]
(using the defining property of the adjoint). If $\hat A=\hat A^\dagger$, then $z^*=z$.
A complex number equal to its conjugate is real. Hence $z\in\mathbb R$.
\end{document}
```

***

## **C2 ‚Äî Unitary preserves inner products (proof)**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-C2 Unitary invariance ‚Äî Problem}
Prove that if $\hat U^\dagger \hat U=I$, then $\langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle=\langle\phi|\psi\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Write $\langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle=\langle\phi|U^\dagger U|\psi\rangle$.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Interpret: unitaries preserve transition probabilities.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
\[
\langle U\phi|U\psi\rangle = \langle\phi|U^\dagger U|\psi\rangle = \langle\phi|I|\psi\rangle=\langle\phi|\psi\rangle.
\]
\end{document}
```

***

## **C3 ‚Äî Basis-change law $$A'=UAU^\dagger$$ (proof)**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-C3 Basis-change transformation ‚Äî Problem}
Let $|n'\rangle=\sum_m U_{mn}|m\rangle$ with $U$ unitary. Show that the matrix of $\hat A$ in the primed basis is
$A'=UAU^\dagger$, i.e. $A'_{ij}=\langle i'|\hat A|j'\rangle$ equals $(UAU^\dagger)_{ij}$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Write $\langle i'|=\sum_p U_{pi}^*\langle p|$.
\item Substitute into $\langle i'|\hat A|j'\rangle$ and rearrange sums.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Recognize $\sum_{p,q} U_{pi}^* A_{pq} U_{qj}$ as $(U^\dagger A U)_{ij}$ or $(UAU^\dagger)_{ij}$ depending on convention; check indices carefully.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
We compute
\[
A'_{ij}=\langle i'|\hat A|j'\rangle
=\left(\sum_p U_{pi}^*\langle p|\right)\hat A\left(\sum_q U_{qj}|q\rangle\right)
=\sum_{p,q} U_{pi}^*\,\langle p|\hat A|q\rangle\,U_{qj}.
\]
But $A_{pq}=\langle p|\hat A|q\rangle$, so
\[
A'_{ij}=\sum_{p,q} (U^\dagger)_{ip}\,A_{pq}\,U_{qj}=(U^\dagger A U)_{ij}.
\]
If you define the basis change oppositely (new coords $c'=Uc$), you obtain $A'=UAU^\dagger$.
Both are consistent once you fix the convention. The invariant statement is: the operator matrix transforms by unitary similarity.
\end{document}
```

***

## **C4 ‚Äî Projector algebra + interpretation**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry,amsmath,amssymb,physics}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-C4 Projectors ‚Äî Problem}
Let $P=|a\rangle\langle a|$ with $\langle a|a\rangle=1$.
(i) Prove $P^2=P$ and $P^\dagger=P$.
(ii) Show that for any $|\psi\rangle$, $P|\psi\rangle=|a\rangle\langle a|\psi\rangle$ is the component of $|\psi\rangle$ along $|a\rangle$.

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Use dyad multiplication and normalization.
\item Interpret $\langle a|\psi\rangle$ as a scalar coefficient.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Show $P$ acts as identity on the span of $|a\rangle$ and annihilates orthogonal vectors.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution}
(i) Same as S7:
\[
P^2=|a\rangle(\langle a|a\rangle)\langle a|=|a\rangle\langle a|=P,\quad
P^\dagger=|a\rangle\langle a|=P.
\]
(ii)
\[
P|\psi\rangle = |a\rangle\langle a|\psi\rangle.
\]
This is a scalar multiple of $|a\rangle$, hence lies in the subspace spanned by $|a\rangle$.
If $|\psi\rangle$ is orthogonal to $|a\rangle$, then $\langle a|\psi\rangle=0$ and $P|\psi\rangle=0$.
So $P$ extracts the $|a\rangle$ component.
\end{document}
```

***

## **C5 ‚Äî Hermitian vs self-adjoint (MSc/PhD structured essay)**

```latex
\documentclass[11pt]{article}
\usepackage{geometry}
\geometry{margin=1in}
\begin{document}

\section*{L03-C5 Hermitian vs self-adjoint ‚Äî Problem}
Write a structured 1--2 page essay answering:
\begin{enumerate}
\item In physics language, what do we call a Hermitian operator and why?
\item In mathematics, what is the distinction between Hermitian (symmetric) and self-adjoint?
\item Why does this matter for observables like position and momentum?
\end{enumerate}

\newpage
\section*{Suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Focus on the idea of domains for unbounded operators.
\item Keep it conceptual; no heavy proofs required.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Advanced suggestions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Give an example statement: ``an operator can be symmetric on a domain but fail to have a self-adjoint extension'' (or require boundary conditions).
\item Connect self-adjointness to real spectrum and unitary time evolution.
\end{itemize}

\newpage
\section*{Detailed solution (model answer outline)}
A high-quality answer should:
\begin{itemize}
\item Explain that physics often uses ``Hermitian'' informally for ``self-adjoint''.
\item State that for unbounded operators, the domain is part of the definition; $\hat A^\dagger$ may have a different domain.
\item Note that self-adjointness ensures real spectral values and generates unitary evolution (via Stone's theorem, later lecture).
\item Mention that boundary conditions (e.g. particle in a box) correspond to choosing a self-adjoint extension.
\end{itemize}

\end{document}
```

# C) Lecture notes (Narrative + derivations)

## C1) Sakurai track ‚Äî measurement and eigenstates

### Ideal measurement

An **ideal measurement** of observable $$A$$:

*   has possible outcomes $$a$$
*   leaves the system in a state compatible with that outcome

Empirically:

*   repeat measurement ‚Üí same result
*   thus post-measurement state must be an **eigenstate**

***

## C2) Dirac track ‚Äî eigenvalue equation

### Definition

A ket $$|a\rangle$$ is an eigenstate of $$\hat A$$ with eigenvalue $$a$$ if:

$$
\hat A|a\rangle = a|a\rangle
$$

Matrix form:

$$
A\mathbf v = a\mathbf v
$$

***

## C3) Degeneracy

### Definition

An eigenvalue $$a$$ is **degenerate** if there are multiple linearly independent eigenstates:

$$
\hat A|a,\alpha\rangle = a|a,\alpha\rangle,\quad \alpha=1,\dots,g
$$

*   The eigenspace is a **subspace**
*   Measurement outcome tells you $$a$$, **not** which $$\alpha$$

***

## C4) Projectors

### Non‚Äëdegenerate projector

$$
P_a = |a\rangle\langle a|
$$

### Degenerate projector

$$
P_a = \sum_{\alpha=1}^g |a,\alpha\rangle\langle a,\alpha|
$$

### Properties

$$
P_a^2 = P_a,\quad P_a^\dagger = P_a
$$

$$
P_a P_b = 0 \quad (a\neq b)
$$

***

## C5) Spectral decomposition (discrete)

For a Hermitian operator:

$$
\boxed{\hat A = \sum_a a\,P_a}
$$

This is the **operator form** of ‚Äúdiagonalization‚Äù.

Matrix form:

$$
A = \sum_a a \sum_{\alpha}|a,\alpha\rangle\langle a,\alpha|
$$

***

## C6) Probabilities

If the system is in state $$|\psi\rangle$$, probability of outcome $$a$$ is:

$$
P(a)=\langle\psi|P_a|\psi\rangle
$$

***

## C7) L√ºders rule (state update)

### Non‚Äëdegenerate

$$
|\psi\rangle \;\longrightarrow\;
\frac{P_a|\psi\rangle}{\sqrt{\langle\psi|P_a|\psi\rangle}}
$$

### Degenerate (L√ºders rule)

$$
\boxed{
|\psi\rangle \;\longrightarrow\;
\frac{P_a|\psi\rangle}{\sqrt{\langle\psi|P_a|\psi\rangle}}
\quad\text{with } P_a=\sum_\alpha |a,\alpha\rangle\langle a,\alpha|
}
$$

**Key idea:**

*   measurement collapses **onto the eigenspace**, not a particular eigenvector

***

## C8) Synthesis (Sakurai ‚Üî Dirac ‚Üî Matrix)

| Measurement (Sakurai) | Abstract (Dirac) | Matrix                |
| --------------------- | ---------------- | --------------------- |
| outcome $$a$$         | eigenvalue       | eigenvalue            |
| compatible state      | eigenstate       | eigenvector           |
| degeneracy            | subspace         | eigenspace            |
| repeatability         | projection       | idempotence           |
| state update          | L√ºders rule      | normalized projection |

***

# D) Concept Questions (CQ) ‚Äî 10

1.  Why must post‚Äëmeasurement states be eigenstates?
2.  What is degeneracy physically?
3.  Why does a degenerate measurement not determine a unique state?
4.  What is the difference between $$|a\rangle$$ and $$|a,\alpha\rangle$$?
5.  Why are projectors idempotent?
6.  How does a projector differ from an observable?
7.  Why is spectral decomposition representation‚Äëindependent?
8.  Why does L√ºders rule project onto a subspace?
9.  What information is lost in a degenerate measurement?
10. How does this prepare for continuous spectra?

***
